    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="4260a488a4964dcfb9045287a396e920">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '9e964b85-20d0-4227-afd7-7e8dcb341c44'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

            function riskifiedBeaconLoad() {

                var session_id = '2781F2FA48FC07E777BE8BEAF2C30777';

                s.src = url;

                x.parentNode.insertBefore(s, x);
            }

                window.attachEvent('onload', riskifiedBeaconLoad)

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d174618429" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Prussian Blue Analogs (PBAs) have shown considerable prospects as the cathode materials for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) on account of their high theoretical capacity, low cost, and easy preparation. The low practical capacity and poor cycling stability of PBAs, however, limit their wide practical deployment. To address these issues, a new strategy is deployed through introducing Fe vacancies and Cu-dopant into the PBAs frame structure, boosting the specific capacity and improving the cycling stability. On the one hand, the introduction of Fe vacancies in PBA (PBAFe) can provide additional storage sites and facilitate Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics, yielding a high capacity of 164&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>1</sup> at a low current density which approaches the theoretical capacity. On the other hand, the Cu-dopant is further helpful in stabilizing the structure of PBAFe, thus improving the cycling stability. This work paves a new strategy that enables unlocking the capacity and improving the cycling stability of PBA materials toward high-performance SIBs.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202314167-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202314167-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>In response to the sustainable development strategy, lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) have gained widespread applications in energy storage systems in recent years.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314167-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314167-bib-0001_R_d174618310e399" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Energy Environ. Sci.">1</a>, <a href="#adfm202314167-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314167-bib-0002_R_d174618310e399" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Front. Energy Res.">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As an alternative to LIBs, sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) become increasingly popular on account of the low industrialization cost and abundant sodium resource.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314167-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314167-bib-0003_R_d174618310e404" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Nat. Rev. Mater.">3</a>, <a href="#adfm202314167-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314167-bib-0004_R_d174618310e404" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Energy Environ. Sci.">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The cathodes play a significant role in determining the working potential and capacity of SIBs, which in turn affects their energy densities.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314167-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314167-bib-0005_R_d174618310e409" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Adv. Energy Mater.">5</a>, <a href="#adfm202314167-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314167-bib-0006_R_d174618310e409" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Chem. Rev.">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> A variety of cathode materials for SIBs have been explored, including layered metal oxides, polyanionic compounds, and Prussian Blue Analogs (PBAs).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314167-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314167-bib-0007_R_d174618310e415" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - J. Power Sources">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The layered metal oxides possess a high redox potential, but are subjected to poor cyclic performance due to the crystal structural amorphization in the sodiation/desodiation processes, causing a complex phase transformation and structural degradation.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314167-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314167-bib-0008_R_d174618310e420" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Polyanionic compounds possess high structural stability, but the electronic conductivity and ion diffusion kinetics are very low, resulting in the large polarization of electrochemical reactions and poor rate capability.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314167-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314167-bib-0009_R_d174618310e426" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Adv. Energy Mater.">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In contrast, PBAs have a stable open framework that could maintain structural stability and integrity during the sodiation/desodiation processes. Besides, the large open framework structure of PBAs provides abundant 3D diffusion channels for Na<sup>+</sup>, favoring the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314167-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314167-bib-0010_R_d174618310e435" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Coord. Chem. Rev.">10</a>, <a href="#adfm202314167-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314167-bib-0011_R_d174618310e435" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Adv. Energy Mater.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Typically, PBAs have the chemical formula of Na<sub>x</sub>M<sub>1</sub>[M<sub>2</sub>(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sub>y</sub>nH<sub>2</sub>O (0 &lt; <i>x</i> &lt; 2 and 0 &lt; <i>y</i> &lt; 1; M = Fe, Co, Ni, and Cu).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314167-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314167-bib-0012_R_d174618310e459" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In the case of Fe-based PBA (M<sub>1</sub> = M<sub>2</sub> = Fe), Fe is divided into the high-spin Fe (Fe<sup>HS</sup>, M<sub>1</sub>) and the low-spin Fe (Fe<sup>LS</sup>, M<sub>2</sub>), coordinating with CN through N atoms and C atoms, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314167-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314167-bib-0013_R_d174618310e478" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Active Fe<sup>HS</sup> provides the main capacity contribution for the Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation, because Fe<sup>HS</sup> can be effectively reacted with alkali metals.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314167-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314167-bib-0014_R_d174618310e489" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - J. Mater. Chem. A">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In contrast, Fe<sup>LS</sup> is challenging to be activated because Fe<sup>LS</sup> is tightly bounded to carbon atoms with a limited capacity contribution during the sodiation process.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314167-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314167-bib-0015_R_d174618310e499" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">15</a>, <a href="#adfm202314167-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314167-bib-0016_R_d174618310e499" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Nano Energy">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In recent years, extensive studies have been conducted to improve the cycling stability of PBAs. Jiang et&nbsp;al. prepared a Prussian Blue material with low-defect and used sodium carboxymethylcellulose as a moderate chelating agent, which exhibits a capacity of 101&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>1</sup> and a good cycling capacity retention.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314167-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314167-bib-0017_R_d174618310e507" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Nano Energy">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Peng et&nbsp;al. proposed a facile ice-assisted strategy and obtained highly crystallized PBAs, exhibiting a high capacity with an initial Coulombic efficiency of 87% and a long cycling lifespan of 3000 cycles through suppressing structure defects.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314167-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314167-bib-0018_R_d174618310e512" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Nano Lett.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In addition, Huang and co-workers synthesized an innovative border-rich Prussian Blue material with a specific discharge capacity of 120&nbsp;mA&nbsp;h&nbsp;g<sup>1</sup>, which also could maintain 80% capacity over 280 cycles.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314167-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314167-bib-0019_R_d174618310e520" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Nano Energy">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, these studies mainly focus on the enhancement of cyclic stability with limited capacity, the increase of capacity of PBAs remains a challenge.</p>
         
         <p>In this work, a novel synergistic strategy is deployed to synchronously boost the capacity and improve the cyclic stability of PBAs by introducing Fe vacancies and Cu-dopant into the structure through a slow co-precipitation method. On the one hand, the vacancy-containing PBA material Na<sub>1.54</sub>Fe<sub>0.67</sub><sub>0.33</sub>[Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]2.21H<sub>2</sub>O (denoted as PBAFe) showed an initial discharge capacity of 164&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>1</sup> at the current density of 20&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>1</sup>, approaching to the theoretical capacity (170&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>1</sup>). Indeed, the presence of iron vacancy affects the Na<sup>+</sup> transport property and kinetics, thus favoring Na storage capacity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314167-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314167-bib-0020_R_d174618310e547" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Adv. Energy Mater.">20</a>, <a href="#adfm202314167-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314167-bib-0021_R_d174618310e547" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Acc. Chem. Res.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> On the other hand, the electrochemically inactive Cu doping into the defective PBAFe frame structure (dented as PBA(Cu)Fe) can further stabilize the structure over the redox processes (Fe<sup>3+</sup>/Fe<sup>2+</sup>) of Fe<sup>HS</sup> and Fe<sup>LS</sup>,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314167-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314167-bib-0012_R_d174618310e561" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">12</a>, <a href="#adfm202314167-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314167-bib-0022_R_d174618310e561" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> leading to a significant improvement of capacity retention over 200 cycles. Notably, both PBAFe and PBA(Cu)Fe exhibited enhanced diffusion dynamics during the Na<sup>+</sup> extraction and insertion processes. In addition, PBAFe and PBA(Cu)Fe displayed high energy density in the full-cell configuration. The abovementioned results demonstrated a promising strategy for boosting the capacity and improving the cycling stability of PBA materials toward high-performance SIBs.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202314167-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202314167-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202314167-sec-0030">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202314167-sec-0030-title">2.1 Material Characterization</h3>
            
            <p>PBA and vacancy-containing PBAs (PBAFe and PBA(Cu)Fe) materials were synthesized through a co-precipitation method.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314167-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314167-bib-0023_R_d174618310e583" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Appl. Surf. Sci.">23</a>, <a href="#adfm202314167-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314167-bib-0024_R_d174618310e583" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - J. Colloid Interface Sci.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The crystal structure of all these samples has been characterized by X-ray diffraction (XRD). The XRD patterns of PBA and vacancy-containing PBAs reveal similar face-centered cubic symmetry with the space group of <i>Fm</i>-3<i>m</i>, indicating a pure phase structure.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314167-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314167-bib-0022_R_d174618310e592" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">22</a>, <a href="#adfm202314167-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314167-bib-0025_R_d174618310e592" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Energy Storage Mater.">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The specific structure information was further refined by the Rietveld refinements, as shown in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314167-fig-0001">1ac</a>. The refined lattice parameters are a = b = c = 10.25972(4)&nbsp; and V = 1079.95(7)&nbsp;<sup>3</sup> for PBAFe, a = b = c = 10.25713(7)&nbsp; and V = 1079.14(9)&nbsp;<sup>3</sup> for PBA(Cu)Fe, as compared to a = b = c = 10.29892(3)&nbsp; and V = 1092.38(6)&nbsp;<sup>3</sup> for PBA. The other refined parameters, including atomic position and occupancy rate, are listed in Tables <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a><a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a> (Supporting Information). In the framework structure, Fe<sup>HS</sup> is primarily located at the 4b site, while Fe<sup>LS</sup> is located at the 4a site. The C and N are situated at the 24e site, forming a CN bond that connects with Fe<sup>LS</sup> and Fe<sup>HS</sup> (corresponding to Fe<sup>LS</sup>C<sub>6</sub> and Fe<sup>HS</sup>N<sub>6</sub> octahedrons), respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314167-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314167-bib-0026_R_d174618310e634" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Adv. Energy Mater.">26</a>, <a href="#adfm202314167-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314167-bib-0027_R_d174618310e634" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - J. Mater. Chem. A">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Na is mainly located at the 8c site, which is surrounded by Fe<sup>LS</sup>C<sub>6</sub> and Fe<sup>HS</sup>N<sub>6</sub> octahedrons.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314167-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314167-bib-0028_R_d174618310e648" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Inorg. Chem.">28</a>, <a href="#adfm202314167-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314167-bib-0029_R_d174618310e648" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Chem. Commun.">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> For PBAFe (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information), Fe vacancies can be induced through the reaction between high active Fe<sup>HS</sup> ions and acetate ions to break the FeN bonds, while low active Fe<sup>LS</sup> still maintains the coordination with C atoms, the mechanism is illustrated in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> (Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314167-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314167-bib-0014_R_d174618310e664" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - J. Mater. Chem. A">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> For PBA(Cu)Fe, Cu-dopant replaces a small amount of Fe<sup>HS</sup> in the structure (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314167-fig-0001">1g</a>). It's worth noting that the lattice parameter of PBAFe and PBA(Cu)Fe is smaller than that of PBA, which suggests the presence of Fe vacancies that slightly shrink the lattice parameter. The chemical formulas of PBA, PBAFe, and PBA(Cu)Fe determined from the refined results were Na<sub>1.56</sub>Fe[Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>], Na<sub>1.54</sub>Fe<sub>0.67</sub><sub>0.33</sub>[Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>] and Na<sub>1.5</sub>Cu<sub>0.038</sub>Fe<sub>0.691</sub><sub>0.271</sub>[Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>], respectively ( represents Fe vacancies). The element content of PBA, PBAFe, and PBA(Cu)Fe was also determined through the ICP-OES (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information), consistent with refined results. The morphology of PBA, PBAFe, and PBA(Cu)Fe was characterized by SEM (scanning electron microscopy) and TEM (transmission electron microscopy). Both PBA and PBAFe exhibit cubic morphology with the particle size ranging from 40 to 100&nbsp;nm, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314167-fig-0001">1d,e</a>. PBA(Cu)Fe also shows the cubic particle morphology, but with large particle scope from 300 to 400&nbsp;nm (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314167-fig-0001">1f</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information). The differentiated particle size demonstrates that Cu doping is favorable for the formation of regular and larger particles in the PBAs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314167-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314167-bib-0030_R_d174618310e712" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Nat. Energy">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The elements of Na, Fe, Cu, N, and C evenly distributing in the PBA(Cu)Fe (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information) proves the successful incorporation of Cu-dopant.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202314167-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/d7659d50-1712-4de1-929a-f4479ca68d43/adfm202314167-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/d7659d50-1712-4de1-929a-f4479ca68d43/adfm202314167-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/4767305a-e1ff-4461-b3be-6aa9d6da1d33/adfm202314167-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/d7659d50-1712-4de1-929a-f4479ca68d43/adfm202314167-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202314167-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202314167" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Rietveld refinement analysis of a) PBA, b) PBAFe, and c) PBA(Cu)Fe; SEM, TEM diagrams of d) PBA, e) PBAFe, and f) PBA(Cu)Fe; g) structure diagram of PBA(Cu)Fe.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>The electronic structure of PBA, PBAFe, and PBA(Cu)Fe was studied by X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS), the element spectra are plotted in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a> (Supporting Information). As shown in Fe 2p and Cu 2p XPS profiles (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202314167-fig-0002">2a,b</a>), the Fe<sup>2+</sup> state can be represented by the two peaks at 721 and 708&nbsp;eV, and the Fe<sup>3+</sup> state can be represented by the different peaks at 724 and 713&nbsp;eV.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314167-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314167-bib-0031_R_d174618310e756" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Microporous Mesoporous Mater.">31</a>, <a href="#adfm202314167-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314167-bib-0032_R_d174618310e756" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Similarly, the Cu 2p XPS profile of PBA(Cu)Fe shows two different peaks at 955 and 935&nbsp;eV, corresponding to the Cu<sup>2+</sup> state; and two small peaks located at 952 and 933&nbsp;eV representing Cu<sup>+</sup> state, which might be due to the slight reduction of Cu<sup>2+</sup> by EDTA-2Na during the synthesis process.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314167-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314167-bib-0022_R_d174618310e768" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">22</a>, <a href="#adfm202314167-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314167-bib-0033_R_d174618310e768" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - J. Mater. Chem. A">33</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Furthermore, the chemical bond information was investigated by the Fourier-transform infrared (FTIR) and Raman spectra. In the FTIR spectra (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314167-fig-0002">2c</a>), the peaks located at 599 and 2070&nbsp;cm<sup>1</sup> correspond to the stretching vibration of CN and FeCN bonds, respectively; and the peak located at 1610&nbsp;cm<sup>1</sup> matches the coordinated H<sub>2</sub>O in the structure.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314167-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314167-bib-0017_R_d174618310e783" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Nano Energy">17</a>, <a href="#adfm202314167-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314167-bib-0034_R_d174618310e783" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Nanomicro Lett.">34</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Moreover, the peaks located at 520, 2090, and 2150&nbsp;cm<sup>1</sup> in Raman spectra correspond to Fe<sup>LS</sup>CN, CNFe<sup>HS</sup>, and CN bonds, confirming the existence of Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub><sup>4</sup> groups (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314167-fig-0002">2d</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314167-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314167-bib-0019_R_d174618310e801" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Nano Energy">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> N<sub>2</sub> adsorption/desorption test is further used to represent the specific surface area (SSA) of PBA, PBAFe, and PBA(Cu)Fe. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314167-fig-0002">2e</a>, it is revealed that PBAFe (99.35&nbsp;m<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;g<sup>1</sup>) has a much larger SSA than PBA (59.75&nbsp;m<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;g<sup>1</sup>) with a similar particle size. In contrast, the low SSA of PBA(Cu)Fe (42.96&nbsp;m<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;g<sup>1</sup>) is due to the large particle size, which is also consistent with the SEM and TEM results. The thermal stability of PBA, PBAFe, and PBA(Cu)Fe was evaluated through the thermo-gravimetric (TG) analysis. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314167-fig-0002">2f</a>, PBA(Cu)Fe exhibits a lower weight loss compared to PBA and PBAFe between 30 and 400&nbsp;C, indicating a more stable structure of PBA(Cu)Fe through Cu doping. The mass loss between 30 and 250&nbsp;C originates from the adsorbed water, while the mass loss between 250 and 350&nbsp;C is due to the interstitial water.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314167-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314167-bib-0019_R_d174618310e828" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Nano Energy">19</a>, <a href="#adfm202314167-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314167-bib-0035_R_d174618310e828" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Energy Storage Mater.">35</a>-<a href="#adfm202314167-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314167-bib-0037_R_d174618310e828" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Chem. Mater.">37</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Based on the TG analysis combined with the refined XRD and ICP results, the following compositions Na<sub>1.56</sub>Fe[Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]2.74H<sub>2</sub>O, Na<sub>1.54</sub>Fe<sub>0.67</sub><sub>0.33</sub>[Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]2.21H<sub>2</sub>O, and Na<sub>1.5</sub>Cu<sub>0.038</sub>Fe<sub>0.691</sub><sub>0.271</sub>[Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]1.52H<sub>2</sub>O, are established for PBA, PBAFe, and PBA(Cu)Fe, respectively.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202314167-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/cc958844-833b-4b73-954e-aec9dc23a1ea/adfm202314167-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/cc958844-833b-4b73-954e-aec9dc23a1ea/adfm202314167-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/0a7f54a2-f888-45c6-8ee3-98f7ccd2c0d8/adfm202314167-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/cc958844-833b-4b73-954e-aec9dc23a1ea/adfm202314167-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202314167-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202314167" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Fe 2p XPS spectra of PBA and PBAFe; b) Fe 2p and Cu 2p XPS spectra of PBA(Cu)Fe; c) FT-IR spectra, d) Raman spectra, e) BET curve and f) TG curve of PBA, PBAFe, and PBA(Cu)Fe.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
         </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202314167-sec-0040">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202314167-sec-0040-title">2.2 Electrochemical Performance</h3>
            
            <p>The electrochemical performance of PBA, PBAFe, and PBA(Cu)Fe was assessed in a half-cell configuration within 24.2&nbsp;V (vs Na<sup>+</sup> / Na). The cyclic voltammetry (CV) results (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314167-fig-0003">3ac</a>) show two main redox reactions for the Fe<sup>HS</sup> and Fe<sup>LS</sup> during the Na<sup>+</sup> insertion/extraction at the body-center site.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314167-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314167-bib-0034_R_d174618310e906" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Nanomicro Lett.">34</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Indeed, PBA shows two main oxidation peaks at 3.05 and 3.38&nbsp;V; and two reduction peaks located at 2.54 and 3.23&nbsp;V, corresponding to the Fe<sup>3+</sup>/Fe<sup>2+</sup> redox reaction of Fe<sup>HS</sup> and Fe<sup>LS</sup>, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314167-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314167-bib-0034_R_d174618310e920" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Nanomicro Lett.">34</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> For PBAFe, the CV curve displays oxidation peaks at 3.05 and 3.36&nbsp;V, and reduction peaks at 2.58 and 3.24&nbsp;V, respectively; while the CV curve of PBA(Cu)Fe also exhibits the obvious oxidation peaks at 2.98 and 3.36&nbsp;V, and reduction peaks at 2.56 and 3.27&nbsp;V. The oxidation peaks shift to the lower potential while the reduction peaks shift to the higher potential for PBAFe and PBA(Cu)Fe as compared to PBA, suggesting the polarization is reduced.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314167-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314167-bib-0038_R_d174618310e925" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Nano Energy">38</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Additionally, the higher overlapping peaks and fewer side-reaction peaks indicate better reversibility of PBA(Cu)Fe. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314167-fig-0003">3df</a> displays a comparison of the galvanostatic charge-discharge (GCD) curve for PBA, PBAFe, and PBA(Cu)Fe at 20&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>1</sup> current density. Apparently, with the introduction of Fe vacancies, the initial discharge capacity increases from 110 to 163.7&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>1</sup>, which is very approximate to the theoretical capacity of 170&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>1</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314167-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314167-bib-0039_R_d174618310e940" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - Adv. Energy Mater.">39</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a> (Supporting Information) further shows the GCD curves at the current density of 50 and 500&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>1</sup>, PBAFe and PBA(Cu)Fe delivers the discharge capacity of 159.9 and 132.4&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>1</sup> at 50&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>1</sup>, 134.7 and 115.8&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>1</sup> at 500&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>1</sup>, respectively. The capacity comparison at various current densities with previously reported PBA cathode materials is shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a> (Supporting Information), the vacancy-containing PBA in our work exhibits an excellent capacity. Furthermore, the rate performance of PBA, PBAFe, and PBA(Cu)Fe was conducted within the potential at 24.2&nbsp;V (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314167-fig-0003">3g</a>). PBAFe shows the reversible capacity of 166.7, 127.9, 91.3, 78.1, 67.0, and 57.8&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>1</sup> at the current density of 20, 50, 100, 200, 500, and 1000&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>1</sup>, respectively. PBA exhibits the reversible capacities of 106.6, 71.5, 50.9, 41.3, 34.6, and 30.4&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>1</sup>. PBA(Cu)Fe possesses the reversible capacities of 135.7, 110.1, 99.9, 92.8, 84.4, and 77.5&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>1</sup>. Apparently, PBA(Cu)Fe exhibits a smaller capacity decay than that of PBAFe and PBA with the increase of currents. However, even though at a 1000&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>1</sup> high current density, PBA(Cu)Fe could also obtain the reversible capacity of 77.5&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>1</sup> which is higher than PBAFe and PBA, demonstrating a better structural stability and reversibility of PBA(Cu)Fe. Moreover, Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314167-fig-0003">3h</a> shows that PBA(Cu)Fe also exhibits a better cycling performance at the current density of 500&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>1</sup> with a 78.5% cycling capacity retention over 200 cycles compared to 48.4% in PBAFe, demonstrating the cycling stability is significantly improved after Cu doping. Although the capacity of PBA(Cu)Fe is slightly decreased after Cu doping due to the part reduction of the active center, the electrochemically inactive Cu-dopant can stabilize the structure, further buffering the structural degradation over the cycling performance.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314167-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314167-bib-0012_R_d174618310e984" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">12</a>, <a href="#adfm202314167-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314167-bib-0022_R_d174618310e984" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202314167-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/aeb5870e-2f24-440d-b159-7e2753cb74e3/adfm202314167-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/aeb5870e-2f24-440d-b159-7e2753cb74e3/adfm202314167-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/fae97785-079e-41c2-b248-580927f3c9db/adfm202314167-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/aeb5870e-2f24-440d-b159-7e2753cb74e3/adfm202314167-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202314167-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202314167" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical performance in half-cell: CV curves of a) PBA, b) PBAFe, and c) PBA(Cu)Fe at a scan rate of 0.1&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>1</sup>; GCD curves of d) PBA, e) PBAFe, and f) PBA(Cu)Fe at 20&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>1</sup> current density; g) rate performance and h) cycling performance comparison between PBA, PBAFe, and PBA(Cu)Fe at 500&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>1</sup>.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
         </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202314167-sec-0050">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202314167-sec-0050-title">2.3 Kinetics Analysis</h3>
            
            <div class="paragraph-element">A combined analysis such as multi-sweep speed CV, electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS), and galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) were performed to investigate the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics of PBA, PBAFe, and PBA(Cu)Fe materials. Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a> (Supporting Information) depicts the CV curves at the multiple sweep rates of 0.1, 0.3, 0.5, 0.7, and 1&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>1</sup> within 24.2&nbsp;V voltage window. As shown in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314167-fig-0004">4ac</a>, the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics (<i>D</i>) is calculated by linearly fitting the relationship of i<sub>p</sub>v<sup>1/2</sup> with the RandlesSevcik equation (Equation&nbsp;<a class="equationLink scrollableLink" href="#adfm202314167-disp-0001" title="Link to equation">1</a>)<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314167-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314167-bib-0040_R_d174618310e1049" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Nano Energy">40</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup>:

               <div class="inline-equation" id="adfm202314167-disp-0001"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                     <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/56462419-b8e5-4406-9ea5-a302b4af04c2/adfm202314167-math-0001.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:1616301X:media:adfm202314167:adfm202314167-math-0001" display="block" location="graphic/adfm202314167-math-0001.png">
                        <semantics>
                           <mrow>
                              <msub>
                                 <mi>i</mi>
                                 <mi>p</mi>
                              </msub>
                              <mo linebreak="badbreak">=</mo>
                              <mn>2.69</mn>
                              <mo linebreak="goodbreak"></mo>
                              <msup>
                                 <mn>10</mn>
                                 <mn>5</mn>
                              </msup>
                              <msup>
                                 <mi>n</mi>
                                 <mfrac>
                                    <mn>3</mn>
                                    <mn>2</mn>
                                 </mfrac>
                              </msup>
                              <mi>C</mi>
                              <mi>A</mi>
                              <msup>
                                 <mi>D</mi>
                                 <mfrac>
                                    <mn>1</mn>
                                    <mn>2</mn>
                                 </mfrac>
                              </msup>
                              <msup>
                                 <mi>v</mi>
                                 <mfrac>
                                    <mn>1</mn>
                                    <mn>2</mn>
                                 </mfrac>
                              </msup>
                           </mrow>
                           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{equation}{i}_p = 2.69 \times {10}^5{n}^{\frac{3}{2}}CA{D}^{\frac{1}{2}}{v}^{\frac{1}{2}}\end{equation}$$</annotation>
                        </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(1)</span></div>where i<sub>p</sub> is the current peak intensity (mA); n represents the number of electrons per redox reaction; and <i>A</i> refers to the electrode surface area (cm<sup>2</sup>); <i>C</i> is the Na<sup>+</sup> molar concentration in the cathode material (mol&nbsp;cm<sup>3</sup>), and v is the specific scanning rate (mV&nbsp;s<sup>1</sup>). According to Equation&nbsp;<a class="equationLink scrollableLink" href="#adfm202314167-disp-0001" title="Link to equation">1</a> and the slope value of i<sub>p</sub> versus v<sup>1/2</sup>, the diffusion coefficient values <i>D</i><sub>A</sub>, <i>D</i><sub>B</sub>, <i>D</i><sub>C</sub>, <i>D</i><sub>D</sub>, <i>D</i><sub>E</sub>, and <i>D</i><sub>F</sub> of PBA and PBAFe were calculated and listed in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a> (Supporting Information). Moreover, EIS performance was evaluated at 2.9&nbsp;V with corresponding calculated results of PBA, PBAFe, and PBA(Cu)Fe (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314167-fig-0004">4d</a>). The relative parameters are listed in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a> (Supporting Information). The charge transfer resistance (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>) is measured to be 593.9&nbsp; in PBAFe and 658.2&nbsp; in PBA(Cu)Fe compared to 3504&nbsp; in PBA. Consequently, the Warburg coefficient (<sub></sub>) which represents the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion rate inside the electrode materials, was linearly plotted to be 168.35&nbsp;&nbsp;cm<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;s<sup>1/2</sup> in PBAFe, 203.75&nbsp;&nbsp;cm<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;s<sup>1/2</sup> in PBA(Cu)Fe, and 418&nbsp;&nbsp;cm<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;s<sup>1/2</sup> in PBA (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314167-fig-0004">4e</a>). Accordingly, the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient on account of the EIS test was calculated to be 5.00&nbsp;&nbsp;10<sup>10</sup>&nbsp;cm<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;s<sup>1</sup> in PBAFe and 3.42&nbsp;&nbsp;10<sup>10</sup>&nbsp;cm<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;s<sup>1</sup> in PBA(Cu)Fe, compared to 7.12&nbsp;&nbsp;10<sup>11</sup>&nbsp;cm<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;s<sup>1</sup> in PBA, demonstrating a fasted diffusion kinetics in Fe-vacancy containing PBA.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314167-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314167-bib-0017_R_d174618310e1189" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Nano Energy">17</a>, <a href="#adfm202314167-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314167-bib-0041_R_d174618310e1189" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">41</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></div>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202314167-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/345c03e3-45e1-42fb-aa6a-af0b63809c8d/adfm202314167-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/345c03e3-45e1-42fb-aa6a-af0b63809c8d/adfm202314167-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/6157026c-7af7-4642-84cd-d38c079e5890/adfm202314167-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/345c03e3-45e1-42fb-aa6a-af0b63809c8d/adfm202314167-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202314167-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202314167" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical kinetics analysis of PBA, PBAFe and PBA(Cu)Fe: corresponding calculation of CV peak current (i<sub>p</sub>) and scan rate (v) for a) PBA, b) PBAFe, and c) PBA(Cu)Fe at different scan rates between 2 and 4.2&nbsp;V; d) EIS curves and equivalent circuit diagram, and e) the kinetics calculation based on the frequency () and <i>Z</i> values; f) GITT curves versus the number of Na<sup>+</sup> for PBA, PBAFe, and PBA(Cu)Fe measured at current density of 10&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>1</sup> for 10&nbsp;min steps and 3&nbsp;h rest, g) corresponding calculated Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficients.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <div class="paragraph-element">Moreover, the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient (D<sub>Na</sub><sup>+</sup>) upon the Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation/de-intercalation process was testified by GITT for PBAFe, PBA(Cu)Fe, and PBA electrodes. The D<sub>Na+</sub> can be calculated according to the Equation&nbsp;<a class="equationLink scrollableLink" href="#adfm202314167-disp-0002" title="Link to equation">2</a><sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314167-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314167-bib-0042_R_d174618310e1238" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - Adv. Mater.">42</a>, <a href="#adfm202314167-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314167-bib-0043_R_d174618310e1238" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - Energy Environ. Sci.">43</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup>:

               <div class="inline-equation" id="adfm202314167-disp-0002"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                     <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/34bc55e9-c916-4c61-8b3b-0f71990be49c/adfm202314167-math-0002.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:1616301X:media:adfm202314167:adfm202314167-math-0002" display="block" location="graphic/adfm202314167-math-0002.png">

                           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{equation}{D}_{N{a}^ + } = \frac{4}{{\pi \tau }}{(\frac{{{m}_B{V}_M}}{{{M}_BS}})}^2{(\frac{{\Delta {E}_S}}{{\Delta {E}_\tau }})}^2\end{equation}$$</annotation>
                        </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(2)</span></div>where  represents the current pulse time (<i>s</i>); m<sub>B</sub> refers to the mass load (<i>g</i>) of the cathode; and S means the contacting area between electrolyte and electrode materials (cm<sup>2</sup>), which is also equivalent to the cathode-specific surface area; <i>M</i><sub>B</sub> represents the molar mass (g&nbsp;mol<sup>1</sup>), and <i>V</i><sub>M</sub> represents the molar volume (cm<sup>3</sup>&nbsp;mol<sup>1</sup>) of the electrode active materials; <i>E</i><sub></sub> (V) refers to the total changing of potential during a constant current pulse process; <i>E</i><sub>S</sub> (V) represents the voltage difference during the open circuit period.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314167-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314167-bib-0043_R_d174618310e1334" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - Energy Environ. Sci.">43</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Besides, as displayed in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314167-fig-0004">4f,g</a>, the overall Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficients for PBAFe and PBA(Cu)Fe during the charging-discharging process are calculated to 10<sup>10</sup>10<sup>8</sup>&nbsp;cm<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;s<sup>1</sup>, which is much higher than that of PBA (10<sup>13</sup>10<sup>10</sup>&nbsp;cm<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;s<sup>1</sup>). As a result, the higher Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficients in PBAFe and PBA(Cu)Fe during the sodiation/desodiation can boost the electrochemical performance.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314167-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314167-bib-0044_R_d174618310e1365" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - Energy Storage Mater.">44</a>, <a href="#adfm202314167-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314167-bib-0045_R_d174618310e1365" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 45 - Small">45</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></div>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202314167-sec-0060">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202314167-sec-0060-title">2.4 Sodium Storage Mechanism</h3>
            
            <p>Operando XRD measurement was conducted to explore the structural variation of PBA, PBAFe, and PBA(Cu)Fe during the Na<sup>+</sup> de-sodiation and sodiation processes in the potential range of 24.2&nbsp;V (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314167-fig-0005">5</a>). The 2D contour maps display the diffraction peaks of (200), (220), and (400) for PBA, PBAFe, and PBA(Cu)Fe shift continuously during the Na<sup>+</sup> extraction, the (200) diffraction peak is divided into two peaks when the charge voltage increases up to 4.2&nbsp;V, indicating the mixed cubic and tetragonal phases characteristic (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314167-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314167-bib-0032_R_d174618310e1391" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> During the discharge process, (200), (220), and (400) peaks shift back to their initial positions over the Na<sup>+</sup> insertion with a minor presence of a tetragonal phase, revealing the high structural reversibility over the sodiation/desodiation. The schematic illustration is shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a> (Supporting Information). Moreover, the volume evolution of PBAFe, PBA(Cu)Fe, and PBA was calculated and shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314167-fig-0005">5b,d,f</a>. The volume change of PBAFe (2.60%) and PBA(Cu)Fe (2.58%) is much smaller than that of PBA (4.74%), demonstrating the lattice strain is minimalized effectively induced by the presence of Fe vacancies and Cu-dopant.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202314167-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/e8c82e76-8908-427f-ac07-b7dc8f261ed0/adfm202314167-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/e8c82e76-8908-427f-ac07-b7dc8f261ed0/adfm202314167-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/2ac5136d-3850-4922-af41-f33e7e2f38e4/adfm202314167-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/e8c82e76-8908-427f-ac07-b7dc8f261ed0/adfm202314167-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202314167-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202314167" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Operando XRD patterns: real-time charge/discharge curves after activation in the voltage window of 24.2&nbsp;V at 20&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>1</sup> combine with 2D contour plot of (200), (220), and (400) diffraction peaks for a) PBAFe, c) PBA(Cu)Fe, and e) PBA; corresponding volume evolution of b) PBAFe, d) PBA(Cu)Fe, and f) PBA.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Nuclear magnetic resonance (NMR) spectroscopy has been proven to be an appropriate approach to investigate the local structure at the atomic level for materials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314167-bib-0047" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314167-bib-0047_R_d174618310e1429" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 47 - Nat. Commun.">47</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Solid-state <sup>23</sup>Na NMR spectra under magic angle spinning at 14&nbsp;kHz were measured and analyzed for PBAFe and PBA(Cu)Fe electrodes at pristine and various charged/discharged states (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314167-fig-0006">6a</a>). The <sup>23</sup>Na spectrum of pristine PBAFe displays two isotropic signals located at 25.7 and 3.1&nbsp;ppm, and the detailed simulation is shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11a</a> (Supporting Information). The weak signal at 25.7&nbsp;ppm is possibly attributed to the Na<sup>+</sup> next to the vacancy or surface with intensive anisotropy because of two observed SSBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314167-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314167-bib-0048_R_d174618310e1450" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">48</a>, <a href="#adfm202314167-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314167-bib-0049_R_d174618310e1450" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49 - Phys. Rev. Lett.">49</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Additionally, the integral of the signal at 25.7&nbsp;ppm and its SSBs is up to 35%, which further reflects the detected sodium is next to the vacancy since the expected stoichiometry is 33% in PBAFe. When charging to 3.1&nbsp;V, the isotropic signal of PBAFe becomes broad and overlapped, which is possibly caused by the increased disorder. Upon further charging to 4.2&nbsp;V, the signal decreased significantly as expected because of the extracting of Na<sup>+</sup> from the cathode. A reverse trend could be observed for the subsequent discharge process. Interestingly, the cathode shows more SSBs after electrochemical cycling to 2.0&nbsp;V, indicating a larger anisotropy of the Na environment. Correspondingly, the refined ex situ XRD result shows the Na occupancy at various charged/discharged states of PBAFe (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314167-fig-0006">6b</a>). Upon charging to 3.1&nbsp;V, the refined Na occupancy result shows that it remains 1.36 Na<sup>+</sup> ions in the structure, and 1.1 Na<sup>+</sup> ions are further extracted upon charging to 4.2&nbsp;V. When discharging from 4.2&nbsp;V, 0.88 Na<sup>+</sup> ions and 1.48 Na<sup>+</sup> ions are intercalated into the electrode at 3.1 and 2&nbsp;V, respectively.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202314167-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/13ed1190-51c5-45d8-86b7-f8c1806acfb1/adfm202314167-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/13ed1190-51c5-45d8-86b7-f8c1806acfb1/adfm202314167-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/331a943b-ece4-439d-b016-49e18e437362/adfm202314167-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/13ed1190-51c5-45d8-86b7-f8c1806acfb1/adfm202314167-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202314167-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202314167" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Solid-state <sup>23</sup>Na NMR spectra for a) PBAFe and c) PBA(Cu)Fe electrodes at various charge-discharge states. Spinning sidebands (SSBs) are marked with asterisks. Rietveld refinement and corresponding Na occupancy of ex situ XRD data for b) PBAFe and d) PBA(Cu)Fe.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Turns to the PBA(Cu)Fe electrode as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314167-fig-0006">6c</a>, pristine PBA(Cu)Fe shows two resonances at 2.7 and 16&nbsp;ppm. The corresponding simulation is shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11b</a> (Supporting Information), the signal shifting to the high field is attributed to the partially filled vacancy by Cu with a larger radius that offers a stronger shielding effect on Na.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314167-bib-0050" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314167-bib-0050_R_d174618310e1500" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 50 - Chem. Mater.">50</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Both metallic synergistic effects of Fe and Cu, together with influence from the 2nd and 3rd coordination shells, would lead to a smaller resonance separation between the sodium next to the vacancy and the bulky sodium.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314167-bib-0051" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314167-bib-0051_R_d174618310e1505" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 51 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">51</a>-<a href="#adfm202314167-bib-0053" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314167-bib-0053_R_d174618310e1505" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 53 - Chem. Mater.">53</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As the electrodes charge to 3.1&nbsp;V, the spectrum of PBA(Cu)Fe shows 600&nbsp;ppm width of SSBs and broadening isotropic signal. With the charging up to 4.2&nbsp;V, the signal decreases obviously for the Na<sup>+</sup> extraction. In the discharge process, the Na spectra of PBA(Cu)Fe are highly consistent with the one of charging to 3.1&nbsp;V, proving the excellent reversibility. When the cathode is further continuously discharged to 2.0&nbsp;V, it also exhibits larger anisotropy by intercalation and extraction of Na<sup>+</sup>. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314167-fig-0006">6d</a>, the refined Na occupancy result shows that it remains 1.32 Na<sup>+</sup> ions in the structure when charged to 3&nbsp;V, and further extracting 0.98 Na<sup>+</sup> ions during charging to 4.2&nbsp;V. When discharging from 4.2&nbsp;V, the structure sodiates 0.8 Na<sup>+</sup> ions at 3.1&nbsp;V and 1.36 Na<sup>+</sup> ions at 2&nbsp;V, respectively.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202314167-sec-0070">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202314167-sec-0070-title">2.5 Full-Cell Performance</h3>
            
            <p>To further evaluate the practical application of PBAFe and PBA(Cu)Fe in SIBs, the full-cell configurations were constructed with PBAFe/PBA(Cu)Fe as the cathode and hard carbon as the anode (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314167-fig-0007">7a</a>). Galvanostatic curves for PBAFe /PBA(Cu)Fe half-cell configurations and hard carbon half-cell configuration are displayed in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314167-fig-0007">7b,c</a>. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314167-fig-0007">7d</a>, the full cell was measured between 1.5 and 3.8&nbsp;V with various current densities of 50, 200, and 500&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>1</sup>, respectively. PBAFe and PBA(Cu)Fe full-cells exhibit the discharge capacity of 110.6 and 86.3&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>1</sup> at 50&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>1</sup>, respectively. For a sake comparison, the PBA full-cell only exhibits a low capacity of 58.1&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>1</sup> at 50&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>1</sup> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a>, Supporting Information). Additionally, even at the 500&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>1</sup> current density, the PBAFe and PBA(Cu)Fe full-cells exhibit a much higher capacity compared to PBA. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202314167-fig-0007">7e</a>, PBAFe and PBA(Cu)Fe full-cells display a higher energy density than other full-cells with different reported materials (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202314167-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314167-bib-0040_R_d174618310e1572" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Nano Energy">40</a>, <a href="#adfm202314167-bib-0054" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314167-bib-0054_R_d174618310e1572" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 54 - ACS Appl. Energy Mater.">54</a>-<a href="#adfm202314167-bib-0060" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202314167-bib-0060_R_d174618310e1572" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 60 - Energy Storage Mater.">60</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Furthermore, PBA(Cu)Fe full-cell shows a better cycling stability than that of PBAFe with the coulombic efficiency of 98% (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202314167-fig-0007"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/a557f805-037d-4a65-96e8-fcf3f7bb2fb5/adfm202314167-fig-0007-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/a557f805-037d-4a65-96e8-fcf3f7bb2fb5/adfm202314167-fig-0007-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/5b56cd36-720c-4845-afcc-1e71ecddfc78/adfm202314167-fig-0007-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/a557f805-037d-4a65-96e8-fcf3f7bb2fb5/adfm202314167-fig-0007-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 7<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202314167-fig-0007&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202314167" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">PBAFe and PBA(Cu)Fe//Hard carbon full-cell: a) illustrate diagram; positive and negative electrode matching diagram of b) PBAFe//Hard carbon and c) PBA(Cu)Fe//Hard carbon full-cell; and d) GCD curves comparison between 1.5 and 3.8&nbsp;V at 50, 200, and 500&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>1</sup> current density; e) comparison of voltage, specific capacity and energy density between this work and other cathode materials.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
         </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202314167-sec-0080">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202314167-sec-0080-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, a novel synergistic strategy is deployed to simultaneously boost the capacity and improve the cycling stability of PBA by introducing Fe vacancies and Cu-dopant into the structure. On the one hand, the PBAFe cathode exhibits a high discharge capacity of 164&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>1</sup> that is close to theoretical capacity, while providing the additional Na<sup>+</sup> storage sites and boosting the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion. On the other hand, Cu doping into PBAFe is demonstrated to improve the cycling stability, yielding a higher capacity retention of 78.5% compared to 48.4% in PBAFe over 200 cycles at high current density. Moreover, both Fe-vacancy containing PBA (PBAFe and PBA(Cu)Fe) display a high energy density in the full-cell configurations. This work provides a novel path for enhancing the capacity and improving the cycling stability of PBA cathode materials toward high-performance SIBs.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202314167-sec-0090">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202314167-sec-0090-title">4 Experimental Section</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202314167-sec-0100">
            
            <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202314167-sec-0110">
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202314167-sec-0120">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adfm202314167-sec-0120-title"> Synthesis Method</h4>
                  
                  <p>PBA and PBAFe samples were synthesized by the simple co-precipitation method. Typically, stoichiometric Na<sub>4</sub>Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub> and ethylenediaminetetraacetic acid disodium salt (EDTA-2Na) were dissolved in 80&nbsp;mL saturated NaCl solution as solution A. FeCl<sub>3</sub>6H<sub>2</sub>O was dissolved in 60&nbsp;mL saturated NaCl solution as solution B. After dissolving, solution B was slowly dropwise added to the prepared solution A under a magnetic stirring, the co-precipitation process is at N<sub>2</sub> atmosphere and 60&nbsp;C for 6&nbsp;h. Then the obtained precipitates were centrifuged several times with water and ethanol, drying in a vacuum at 60&nbsp;C. And the obtained original sample was denoted as PBA. For comparison, after the above co-precipitation reaction, the supernatant liquor after standing for 2&nbsp;h was removed and 150&nbsp;mL acetic acid solution (CH<sub>3</sub>COOH, 50&nbsp;mL acetic acid, and 100&nbsp;mL water) was added, stirring at 60&nbsp;C for 12&nbsp;h. Then the obtained precipitates were also centrifuged several times with water and ethanol, drying in a vacuum at 60&nbsp;C. Then the sample was denoted as PBAFe.</p>
                  
                  <p>PBA(Cu)Fe sample was also synthesized by a coprecipitation method. Stoichiometric Na<sub>4</sub>Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub> and EDTA-2Na were dissolved in 80&nbsp;mL saturated NaCl solution as solution A. CuCl<sub>2</sub> and FeCl<sub>3</sub>6H<sub>2</sub>O were dissolved in 60&nbsp;mL saturated NaCl solution as solution B. The preparation process is the same as PBAFe. Finally, the obtained samples were denoted as PBA(Cu)Fe.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202314167-sec-0130">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adfm202314167-sec-0130-title"> Materials Characterization</h4>
                  
                  <p>The elemental analysis of all samples was determined by ICP-OES (Agilent ICP-OES 730) and elemental analyzer (EA, German Elementar UNICUBE). The morphology of all these samples was characterized by scanning electron microscopy (SEM, Zeiss Supra55 type) and transmission electron microscopy (TEM, JEOL 2100F). The structural information was collected by X-ray diffraction (XRD, Bruker D8 with the Cu K<sub></sub> radiation,  = 1.5406&nbsp;) at 2<i></i> angles from 10 to 70 with a 0.02 step increment. Rietveld refinement was performed using the Fullprof program. The thermogravimetric analysis (TG) is tested on the thermogravimetric analyzer (NETZSCH STA 449C), under a pure N<sub>2</sub> atmosphere at the constant heating rate of 10&nbsp;K&nbsp;min<sup>1</sup> from 30 to 400&nbsp;C. Raman test was carried out by Raman spectroscopy (Thermo Scientific DXR 2xi Micro-Raman), and Fourier transform infrared (FT-IR) spectra were tested by the FT-IR Microscope (Bruker VERTEX 70). X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) tests were carried out by Escalab 250Xi, Thermo Scientific, using Al K source. The specific surface area was studied through the adsorption/desorption measurements in N<sub>2</sub> (BET, American Micromeritics 3flex).</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202314167-sec-0140">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adfm202314167-sec-0140-title"> Electrochemical Tests</h4>
                  
                  <p>The electrochemical performance of all samples was tested by using the CR2025 type coin-cells. The obtained active materials, acetylene black, and polyvinylidene fluoride (PVDF, 5&nbsp;wt.%) dissolving in n-methyl-2-pyrrolidone (NMP), were mixed and fully dissolved in NMP solvent in a certain mass ratio of 7:2:1. Then the mixture was stirred for 20 min to get the moderate consistency slurry. The slurry was evenly coated on the Al foil and dried at 80&nbsp;C for 12 h in the vacuum. After drying, the working electrode was cut into the 8&nbsp;mm diameter disks (the active material mass loading is 0.8&nbsp;mg&nbsp;cm<sup>2</sup>). Besides, the cells were assembled in the glove box with Ar filled. And the metallic Na foil was used as the counter electrode. NaClO<sub>4</sub> (1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span>) in polycarbonate (PC) with 5&nbsp;vol%. fluoroethylene carbonate (FEC) was used as the electrolyte, and the glass fiber (GF/D) was used as the separator.</p>
                  
                  <p>For constructing the full-cell, negative electrode material hard carbon (HC), acetylene black, and PVDF (5&nbsp;wt.%) dissolved in NMP, mixed, and fully dissolved in NMP solvent in a certain mass ratio of 8:1:1. Then the mixture was stirred for 20 min to get the moderate consistency slurry. It was evenly coated on the Cu foil, then dried at 80&nbsp;C for 12 h in the vacuum. After drying, the electrode was cut into disks of 12&nbsp;mm diameter. The hard carbon was pre-sodiated before assembling the full cell. The electrochemical performance of vacancy-containing PBAs and PBA positive electrodes (P) were further evaluated in the full-cell configuration against hard carbon as the negative electrode (N) with an optimized N/P ratio of 1.7 (data not shown).</p>
                  
                  <p>Galvanostatic chargedischarge (GCD) capacity and rate capability were tested using the NEWARE test system at 30&nbsp;C. Cyclic voltammetry (CV) and electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS, with the frequency range of 100&nbsp;kHz10&nbsp;MHz, amplitude 5&nbsp;mV) were tested at the Bio-Logic VMP-3 electrochemical workstation.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202314167-sec-0150">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adfm202314167-sec-0150-title"> Operando and Ex Situ X-Ray Diffraction (XRD)</h4>
                  
                  <p>Operando and ex situ XRD were tested at room temperature by using the Bruker D8 X-ray diffractometer. For operando XRD, PBA, PBAFe, and PBA(Cu)Fe electrodes were made into the slurry with the ratio consistent to the proportion that same as above in the coin-cells, and then slowly coated on the 10&nbsp;m ultra-thin Al foil with a Be window. The specific data were obtained in the 2 angle range of 1070, with a 15 min constant test time per spectrum. For ex-situ XRD, PBA, PBAFe, and PBA(Cu)Fe electrodes were tested with Be window after cycling at different potentials of 2, 3.1, and 4.2&nbsp;V, respectively. The data were obtained with the 2 angle from 12 to 36.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202314167-sec-0160">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adfm202314167-sec-0160-title"> NMR Characterization</h4>
                  
                  <p>All <sup>23</sup>Na NMR experiments were obtained on a Bruker 600&nbsp;MHz spectrometer (AVANCE NEO). The Larmor frequency for <sup>23</sup>Na was 158.74&nbsp;MHz. The prepared electrodes were filled into 3.2&nbsp;mm rotors in an argon-filled glovebox. The specific spinning rate was set to 14&nbsp;kHz. A single pulse of 2.04&nbsp;s was chosen for 90 to polarize <sup>23</sup>Na spins. 1&nbsp;mol&nbsp;L<sup>1</sup> NaCl solution at 0&nbsp;ppm was used as the <sup>23</sup>Na reference. The spectra were analyzed with Dmfit software.</p>
                  </section>
               </section>
            </section>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">

                  <li data-bib-id="adfm202314167-bib-0005"><span class="bullet">5</span><span class="author">C. Fang</span>, <span class="author">Y. Huang</span>, <span class="author">W.
